import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import shapes
import math

# Reuse the kernel from solution.py
import solution

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

# Test shape 4 with more splits
shape = shapes.SHAPES[4]
apply_shape(shape)

torch.manual_seed(42)
B = shape["batch"]
H = shape["num_heads"]
Hkv = shape["num_kv_heads"]
D = shape["head_dim"]
L = shape["seq_len"]
P = shape["page_size"]

pages_per_seq = (L + P - 1) // P
total_pages = max(B * pages_per_seq + 8, 64)

query = torch.randn(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
kv_cache = torch.randn(total_pages, P, Hkv, 2 * D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
block_table = torch.randperm(total_pages)[:B * pages_per_seq].reshape(B, pages_per_seq).int().to(device)
seq_lens_tensor = torch.full((B,), L, dtype=torch.int32, device=device)

sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(D)

ref_model = reference.Model(B, H, Hkv, D, L, P).to(device).eval()
with torch.no_grad():
    ref_out = ref_model(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor)

for num_kv_splits in [8, 12, 16, 20]:
    try:
        out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
        max_diff = (out - ref_out).abs().max().item()
        
        for _ in range(10):
            out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        
        times = []
        for _ in range(30):
            _l2_scratch.zero_()
            torch.cuda.synchronize()
            s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
            e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
            s.record()
            out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
            e.record()
            torch.cuda.synchronize()
            times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
        
        import statistics
        print(f"splits={num_kv_splits:2d} ms={statistics.median(times):.3f} max_diff={max_diff:.4f}")
    except Exception as e:
        print(f"splits={num_kv_splits} ERROR: {e}")

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import shapes
import math
import solution

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

for shape_idx in range(5):
    shape = shapes.SHAPES[shape_idx]
    apply_shape(shape)
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    B = shape["batch"]
    H = shape["num_heads"]
    Hkv = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    D = shape["head_dim"]
    L = shape["seq_len"]
    P = shape["page_size"]
    
    pages_per_seq = (L + P - 1) // P
    total_pages = max(B * pages_per_seq + 8, 64)
    
    query = torch.randn(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
    kv_cache = torch.randn(total_pages, P, Hkv, 2 * D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
    block_table = torch.randperm(total_pages)[:B * pages_per_seq].reshape(B, pages_per_seq).int().to(device)
    seq_lens_tensor = torch.full((B,), L, dtype=torch.int32, device=device)
    
    sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(D)
    
    ref_model = reference.Model(B, H, Hkv, D, L, P).to(device).eval()
    with torch.no_grad():
        ref_out = ref_model(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor)
    
    print(f"\nshape={shape_idx} {shape}")
    for num_kv_splits in [1, 2, 4, 8, 12, 16, 20]:
        try:
            out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
            max_diff = (out - ref_out).abs().max().item()
            if max_diff > 0.02:
                continue
            
            for _ in range(10):
                out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
            torch.cuda.synchronize()
            
            times = []
            for _ in range(30):
                _l2_scratch.zero_()
                torch.cuda.synchronize()
                s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
                e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
                s.record()
                out = solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens_tensor, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
                e.record()
                torch.cuda.synchronize()
                times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
            
            import statistics
            print(f"  splits={num_kv_splits:2d} ms={statistics.median(times):.3f}")
        except Exception as e:
            pass

What if I use a fixed partition size of 128 or 256 tokens? Stage 1 = 256 blocks. Good. - Shape 4: ceil(2000/256) = 8 blocks. Stage 1 = 256 blocks. But optimal was 512 blocks. Actually, my current heuristic already gives good block counts. The issue for shape 4 is that even with 512 blocks, performance is only 19%. Wait, let me think about this differently. For shape 4: - D=64, so each K row is 128 bytes = 1 cache line - Each page has 16 tokens, so K load is 16 cache lines spaced 1024 bytes apart - For 125 tokens, that's ~8 pages = 128 cache lines - Total K+V per block = 256 cache lines = 32 KB - With 512 blocks, total memory = 16 MB - Time = 0.034 ms - Effective bandwidth = 16 MB / 0.034 ms = 470 GB/s = 26% peak Why is effective bandwidth so low? The issue is the scattered cache line loads. For each token, K is one 128-byte cache line and V is another 128-byte cache line. But K and V for the same token are adjacent (K at offset 0, V at offset 128 within the token's 256-byte chunk). Wait, for D=64, each token's K+V is 256 bytes. K is bytes [0, 127], V is bytes [128, 255]. For token t, the base address is `page_base + t * stride_kv1`. For num_kv_heads=4, stride_kv1 = 4 * 2 * 64 * 2 = 1024 bytes. Wait, stride_kv1 is in elements, not bytes! In the kernel, `offs_token_in_page[:, None] * stride_kv1 + offs_d[None, :]`. `stride_kv1` is the number of elements between consecutive tokens in a page. For kv_cache shape (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, 2*head_dim): - stride_1 = num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim = 4 * 128 = 512 elements - Wait, 2 * head_dim = 128 elements for D=64. - stride_1 = 4 * 128 = 512 elements In bytes: 512 elements * 2 bytes/element = 1024 bytes. So K for token 0: bytes [0, 127] K for token 1: bytes [1024, 1151] K for token 2: bytes [2048, 2175] ... Each K row is 128 bytes (1 cache line). Consecutive K rows are 1024 bytes apart. For 16 tokens, we load 16 cache lines spaced 1024 bytes apart. Total span = 15 * 1024 + 128 = 15,488 bytes ≈ 15 KB. The memory controller can issue multiple requests in parallel. But 16 scattered requests per page is a lot. For V, same pattern: 16 cache lines spaced 1024 bytes apart, starting at byte 128. In total, for one page, we issue 32 scattered cache line requests spanning ~15.5 KB. Compare to contiguous layout: 32 cache lines spanning 4 KB (contiguous). The contiguous layout would be ~4x more efficient. So the packed layout is costing us a factor of 3-4 in memory bandwidth. This explains why shape 4 is at ~20% instead of ~80%. To fix this, I'd need to either: 1. Pre-process the KV cache into a contiguous layout 2. Use a custom CUDA kernel that loads full pages into shared memory efficiently 3. Accept the limitation and optimize other aspects For option 1, pre-processing adds overhead. But for decode, if we process many tokens, the pre-processing cost is amortized. In our benchmark, we only process one forward pass at a time. So pre-processing would add extra memory traffic. For option 2, a custom CUDA kernel could: 1. Load 16 tokens' K+V data (4096 bytes) into shared memory using all threads in the block 2. Reorganize in shared memory so K and V are contiguous 3. Compute dot products using the reorganized data This would be much more efficient. But writing this kernel is complex. Actually, there's a simpler CUDA kernel approach. Since each page is 4096 bytes for D=64, and a warp has 32 threads, each thread loads 128 bytes (2 float4s). This is perfectly coalesced if threads load consecutive chunks. Wait, the page data for one token and one head is 256 bytes. For 16 tokens, it's 4096 bytes. If 128 threads (4 warps) each load 32 bytes (a float4), that's 128 * 32 = 4096 bytes. Perfect coalescing! Then in shared memory, we can lay out K and V contiguously for all 16 tokens. Then each warp computes the dot products. This is essentially what FlashInfer does. And it's why FlashInfer is so much faster. But writing this in Triton is hard because Triton doesn't give explicit control over shared memory layout. Let me think about whether I can write a CUDA kernel using `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline`. The kernel would be something like: ```cpp __global__ void paged_attention_kernel( const __nv_bfloat16* query, const __nv_bfloat16* kv_cache, const int* block_table, const int* seq_lens, __nv_bfloat16* output, float scale, int batch, int num_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, int page_size ) { // Each block handles one query head int batch_idx = blockIdx.x; int head_idx = blockIdx.y; int kv_head = head_idx / (num_heads / num_kv_heads); int seq_len = seq_lens[batch_idx]; // Load query into registers/shared memory __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 q_shared[128]; // ... // Iterate over pages int num_pages = (seq_len + page_size - 1) / page_size; float e_max = -INFINITY; float e_sum = 0.0f; float acc[128]; // output accumulator for (int page_idx = 0; page_idx < num_pages; ++page_idx) { int physical_page = block_table[batch_idx * max_pages + page_idx]; // Load page into shared memory __shared__ __nv_bfloat16 page_shared[16 * 128]; // 16 tokens * 128 elements // Each thread loads a chunk // Extract K and V // Compute scores // Update softmax } // Write output } ``` This is a lot of code. And I need to handle different head_dims (64 and 128) and page_size=16. Actually, for head_dim=128, each token is 512 bytes. A page of 16 tokens is 8192 bytes. With 128 threads, each loads 64 bytes. This is still coalesced. For head_dim=128, the block size might need to be larger to handle the bigger shared memory. Let me think about whether I can write a simpler kernel. Actually, vLLM has an existing CUDA kernel for paged attention. Maybe I can adapt it. But vLLM's kernel expects a different KV cache layout. Our layout is packed: (num_blocks, page_size, num_kv_heads, head_dim * 2). vLLM uses separate K and V caches with different layouts. Adapting vLLM's kernel to our layout would require modifying the memory access patterns. Actually, what if I just pre-split K and V in Python, then call vLLM's kernel? But vLLM is forbidden! The vLLM kernel is quite sophisticated. It uses: 1. Thread groups to load and compute partial dot products 2. Warp-level reductions 3. Shared memory for logits and query vectors 4. Careful vectorized loads Adapting this to our packed layout would require significant changes. Let me think about whether I can write a simpler but effective CUDA kernel. Actually, let me try a different approach. Instead of a full custom CUDA kernel, what if I use `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` to write a simple kernel that does the following: 1. One thread block per (batch, query_head) 2. Load query into shared memory 3. Iterate over pages, loading K and V into shared memory 4. Compute dot products and softmax 5. Write output For D=128 and PAGE_SIZE=16, each page is 8192 bytes for K+V per head. With 128 threads, each thread loads 64 bytes. This is perfectly coalesced. But the packed layout has K and V interleaved per token. So for token t: - K: offset t * stride_kv1 + 0 - V: offset t * stride_kv1 + HEAD_DIM For PAGE_SIZE=16, stride_kv1 = num_kv_heads * 2 * HEAD_DIM elements. In a CUDA kernel, I can load the full page data as a contiguous chunk and then access K and V elements by computing the correct indices. Actually, if I view the KV cache as a flat array and compute offsets correctly, each page for one head is NOT contiguous. The elements are spaced by stride_kv1. Wait, no. For one (physical_block, kv_head), the memory layout is: ``` [page_size rows, each row is (2 * head_dim) elements] ``` But the rows are contiguous! Because within a block, tokens are stored consecutively: ``` block_base = physical_block * page_size * num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim token_0 = block_base + 0 * num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim + kv_head * 2 * head_dim token_1 = block_base + 1 * num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim + kv_head * 2 * head_dim ``` So for one (block, head), tokens are spaced by `num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim` elements. But within each token, K and V are contiguous. So loading K for all tokens in a page requires strided loads. But loading the full page data (K+V for all tokens) is contiguous in the sense that it's a 2D block with contiguous rows. Wait, actually the full page data for one head is: - Shape: (page_size, 2*head_dim) - Stride between rows: num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim - But we're only reading one head's rows, so the effective stride is num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim This is the same strided layout as before. But in CUDA, I can use a 2D texture or surface load... No, that's overkill. Actually, the simplest approach in CUDA is: 1. Each thread loads one or more K elements and one or more V elements 2. Use shared memory to transpose the data into a more convenient layout For example, with 128 threads and a page of 16 tokens with D=128: - Total K elements = 16 * 128 = 2048 - Total V elements = 16 * 128 = 2048 - Each thread loads 16 K elements and 16 V elements (32 total) But the memory access pattern is: - Thread 0 loads K[0, 0..15], K[1, 0..15], etc. - Thread 1 loads K[0, 16..31], K[1, 16..31], etc. This is similar to what Triton does automatically. The question is whether manual CUDA can do it more efficiently. Actually, I think the real issue is not the load pattern but the small tile sizes for dot products. For D=64 and PAGE_SIZE=16, `tl.dot((8, 64), (64, 16))` is a small MMA. In CUDA, I could use warp-level primitives to compute the dot product more efficiently. For example, I could have each warp compute the dot product for one query head. The warp loads K and V for 16 tokens, computes 16 dot products of 64 elements each using warp shuffle reductions, then does softmax and accumulates V. A warp has 32 threads. For D=64, each thread handles 2 elements of the query and 2 elements of each K vector. The dot product is computed with warp shuffle: ```cpp float qk = 0; for (int d = lane; d < 64; d += 32) { qk += q[d] * k[token][d]; } // Warp reduction for (int mask = 16; mask > 0; mask /= 2) { qk += __shfl_xor_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, qk, mask); } ``` This avoids tensor cores entirely and uses FP32 FMAs. For small D=64, this might actually be faster than tensor cores because there's no setup overhead. For D=128, each thread handles 4 elements. Still efficient. And for the packed layout, each thread can load its assigned elements from K and V directly. Let me try writing this kernel. It would be a single-stage kernel with one warp per query head. For shape 0: 8 batch * 32 heads = 256 warps. With 32 warps per SM (max occupancy), we need 8 SMs. But each warp processes the full sequence of 1024 tokens. This is fine because memory bandwidth is the bottleneck. Wait, with one warp per head, each warp does a lot of sequential work. Memory latency is hidden by having many warps per SM, but with only 256 warps total, we might not fully utilize the GPU. For shape 4: 8 batch * 16 heads = 128 warps. Even fewer. What if I use one block (4 warps) per head? Then: - Shape 0: 256 blocks * 4 warps = 1024 warps - Shape 4: 128 blocks * 4 warps = 512 warps This gives better occupancy. And within the block, the 4 warps can cooperate to load pages. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. In vLLM's kernel, each block handles one head and uses 128 threads (4 warps). The 4 warps iterate over blocks cooperatively. For my custom kernel, I can do something similar: 1. Block size = 128 threads (4 warps) 2. Grid = (num_heads, batch) 3. Each block processes one query head for one batch element 4. Warps iterate over pages in a round-robin fashion For the packed layout: - Load K and V for one page (16 tokens) into shared memory - But the packed layout is strided, so loading into SMEM requires strided loads Actually, what if I don't use shared memory for K and V? What if each thread in the warp loads the K and V elements it needs directly from global memory? For D=128 and PAGE_SIZE=16: - Warp has 32 threads - Each thread is responsible for 4 elements of D (128/32=4) - For each token, thread t loads K[token][t*4:(t+1)*4] and V[token][t*4:(t+1)*4] - Compute partial dot product: sum(q[d] * k[d]) - Warp shuffle to get full dot product The memory access pattern: - For token 0, thread 0 loads K[0][0..3], thread 1 loads K[0][4..7], etc. - These are contiguous! K[0][0..3] is at address page_base + 0*stride + 0 - K[0][4..7] is at address page_base + 0*stride + 4 - etc. So within a token, the loads are perfectly coalesced! And for V, same thing but offset by 128. For consecutive tokens, the address jumps by stride_kv1 = num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim elements. In the kernel, if all 32 threads in the warp load K for the same token, they access contiguous memory. Then they move to the next token. For PAGE_SIZE=16 tokens, the warp processes all 16 tokens, loading K and V for each. This is actually very efficient! The memory access is: 1. Token 0 K: 32 threads load 128 contiguous elements = 2 cache lines 2. Token 0 V: 32 threads load 128 contiguous elements = 2 cache lines 3. Token 1 K: 2 cache lines at +stride_kv1 4. Token 1 V: 2 cache lines at +stride_kv1+128 ... For 16 tokens: 64 cache lines total, spaced by stride_kv1. In Triton, the load pattern is similar but Triton might not optimize it as well as hand-written CUDA. Actually, wait. In Triton, for `k = tl.load(page_base + offs_token[:, None] * stride_kv1 + offs_d[None, :])` with shape (16, 128): - Thread (t, d) accesses page_base + t * stride_kv1 + d - Threads in a warp might be arranged as (0, 0..31), (0, 32..63), etc. - So warp 0 accesses token 0, elements 0..31 (contiguous) - Warp 1 accesses token 0, elements 32..63 (contiguous) - etc. This is the same pattern! So Triton should already be generating efficient loads. Then why is performance so low for shape 4? The issue might be that Triton's `tl.dot` with small matrices has high overhead. For BLOCK_H=8, q is (8, 64), k.T is (64, 16). `tl.dot` computes an 8x16 matrix. But only 4 rows are valid (for group_size=4). So half the computation is wasted. In my CUDA kernel idea, each warp handles exactly one head. No wasted computation. For shape 4 with 128 warps (128 blocks * 1 warp each, or 32 blocks * 4 warps each), each warp processes one head. The dot product is computed with warp shuffle, not tensor cores. Let me estimate the performance: - Each warp processes 2000 tokens - For each token: load K (128 bytes), load V (128 bytes), compute dot (64 FMAs), compute softmax, accumulate V (64 FMAs) - Total memory per warp: 2000 * 256 bytes = 512 KB - Total compute per warp: 2000 * 128 FMAs = 256K FMAs - Memory bound: 512 KB per warp With 128 warps on 188 SMs, each SM gets < 1 warp. That's terrible occupancy! With 512 warps (128 blocks * 4 warps), each SM gets ~2-3 warps. Still not great. So the occupancy issue remains. To get good occupancy, I need more blocks. Which means I need to split the sequence into chunks, like my current Triton approach. What if I use a block per (head, chunk) where chunk = 64 or 128 tokens? - Shape 4: 16 heads * 8 batch * 32 chunks = 4096 blocks! - Each block processes 64 tokens = 4 pages - With 188 SMs, each SM runs ~22 blocks. Excellent occupancy! But then I need a reduction kernel to combine chunks. That's a 2-stage approach, same as my Triton kernel. The key difference would be: 1. Stage 1: each block processes 64 tokens with warp shuffle dot products 2. Stage 2: reduce across chunks For stage 1 with 64 tokens per block and D=64: - 4 warps per block - Warp 0 processes tokens 0..15 - Warp 1 processes tokens 16..31 - Warp 2 processes tokens 32..47 - Warp 3 processes tokens 48..63 Each warp loads K and V for 16 tokens and computes dot products using warp shuffle. This could be much more efficient than Triton because: 1. No tensor core overhead for small matrices 2. Explicit control over memory access 3. No wasted compute (each warp handles exactly 1 head) Let me write this kernel! Actually, let me start with a simpler version. I'll write a single-stage kernel that processes the full sequence for one head per warp. Even with low occupancy, the efficient dot products might compensate. For shape 4: 128 blocks * 4 warps = 512 warps. Each warp does 2000 tokens. Time = ? If memory bandwidth is 1800 GB/s and each warp reads 512 KB, total reads = 512 * 512 KB = 256 MB. But actual data is only 16 MB! Because all warps for the same KV head read the same data. Wait, with 1 warp per head, each of the 16 query heads in shape 4 reads its own K and V data independently. Since group_size=4, 4 query heads share the same KV head. So each KV head's data is read 4 times. Total memory reads = 16 MB * 4 = 64 MB. At 1800 GB/s: 0.036 ms. If the kernel achieves 50% of peak due to scattered loads: 0.072 ms. With the Triton kernel, I'm getting 0.034 ms with splits=20. Wait, the Triton kernel with splits=20 achieves 0.034 ms! That's faster than the theoretical estimate for the CUDA kernel. How? Because with splits=20 and BLOCK_H=8, stage 1 blocks = 8 * 4 * 20 = 640. Each block processes 100 tokens = 6.25 pages. With 640 blocks, the GPU is well utilized. And tl_dot might be more efficient than I thought for small matrices. So maybe I don't need a custom CUDA kernel. Let me just optimize the Triton configuration further. For shape 4, the best I found was splits=20 at 0.033 ms. Let me test even higher splits. import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

for shape_idx in [2, 4]:
    shape = shapes.SHAPES[shape_idx]
    apply_shape(shape)
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
    ref_model = reference.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
    
    torch.manual_seed(42)
    inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]
    
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(10):
        sol_model(*inputs)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    # Time with L2 flush
    times = []
    for _ in range(30):
        _l2_scratch.zero_()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        sol_model(*inputs)
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    
    import statistics
    print(f"shape={shape_idx} {shape} ms={statistics.median(times):.3f}")
    
    # Also time paged_attention_decode directly with the heuristic's num_kv_splits
    import inspect
    src = inspect.getsource(solution.Model.forward)
    # Just call direct
    if shape_idx == 2:
        num_kv_splits = 16
    else:
        num_kv_splits = 16
    
    times2 = []
    for _ in range(30):
        _l2_scratch.zero_()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], 1.0/math.sqrt(shape["head_dim"]), shape["page_size"], num_kv_splits)
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times2.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    
    print(f"  direct splits={num_kv_splits} ms={statistics.median(times2):.3f}")
EOF

import sys

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

shape = shapes.SHAPES[2]

torch.manual_seed(42)
init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

torch.manual_seed(42)
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]

# Warmup both
for _ in range(15):
    sol_model(*inputs)
    solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], 1.0/math.sqrt(128), 16, 16)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Time model
import statistics
times_model = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model(*inputs)
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times_model.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Model: ms={statistics.median(times_model):.3f}")

# Time direct
times_direct = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], 1.0/math.sqrt(128), 16, 16)
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times_direct.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Direct: ms={statistics.median(times_direct):.3f}")

# Time with state dict load
sd = sol_model.state_dict()
sol_model.load_state_dict(sd, strict=True)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

times_post_load = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model(*inputs)
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times_post_load.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Post-load: ms={statistics.median(times_post_load):.3f}")

    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]

    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model(*inputs)
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math
import statistics

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

shape = shapes.SHAPES[4]
apply_shape(shape)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
B = shape["batch"]
H = shape["num_heads"]
Hkv = shape["num_kv_heads"]
D = shape["head_dim"]
L = shape["seq_len"]
P = shape["page_size"]
pages_per_seq = (L + P - 1) // P
total_pages = max(B * pages_per_seq + 8, 64)

query = torch.randn(B, H, D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
kv_cache = torch.randn(total_pages, P, Hkv, 2 * D, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
block_table = torch.randperm(total_pages)[:B * pages_per_seq].reshape(B, pages_per_seq).int().to(device)
seq_lens = torch.full((B,), L, dtype=torch.int32, device=device)
sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(D)

for num_kv_splits in [8, 16, 20]:
    # Warmup
    for _ in range(15):
        solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    times = []
    for _ in range(30):
        _l2_scratch.zero_()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        solution.paged_attention_decode(query, kv_cache, block_table, seq_lens, sm_scale, P, num_kv_splits)
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    
    print(f"splits={num_kv_splits} ms={statistics.median(times):.3f}")

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")

# Temporarily modify solution.Model to not use register_buffer
class TestModel(solution.Model):
    def __init__(self, *args, **kwargs):
        torch.nn.Module.__init__(self)
        assert args[1] % args[2] == 0
        self.batch = args[0]
        self.num_heads = args[1]
        self.num_kv_heads = args[2]
        self.head_dim = args[3]
        self.seq_len = args[4]
        self.page_size = args[5]
        self.group_size = args[1] // args[2]
        import math

reasoning (7041 chars)
Aha! The `state_dict()` of the reference model is empty! Even though `_dummy` is registered as a buffer. Why? `persistent=False` means the buffer is not included in the state dict. So `state_dict()` returns `{}`. And when we call `load_state_dict({}, strict=True)` on any model, it succeeds because there are no keys to load and no missing keys (since the model also has no parameters). So `_dummy` is completely irrelevant! It's just a dummy buffer that doesn't appear in the state dict. We don't even need it. This means the Model overhead is just from the nn.Module call path itself, not from any buffer operations. OK, so nn.Module overhead is ~6 microseconds for this small kernel. That's about 18% for the fastest shape, but less for slower shapes. For the purpose of benchmark.py, this overhead is included in all measurements. The reference model also has the same nn.Module overhead. The compiled model uses CUDA graphs which eliminate Python overhead. The benchmark reports `ms_sol` and compares to the memory bandwidth model. The Python overhead affects the absolute ms but doesn't change the fundamental kernel performance. Now, the real question is: can I improve the kernel performance to get closer to peak bandwidth? Let me think about fundamental limits: 1. The KV cache is accessed through page-table indirection. Each token's page index must be loaded dynamically. 2. The packed layout [K|V] means K and V are stored contiguously in the last dimension, but each page has `page_size * num_kv_heads * 2 * head_dim` elements. 3. For each token, we load `2 * head_dim` bf16 elements (K and V), which is 256 bytes for D=128 or 128 bytes for D=64. 4. With BLOCK_N=16, we load 16 tokens at a time = 4096 bytes for D=128. With 4 warps, each warp handles 4 tokens = 1024 bytes. 5. The memory access pattern for KV cache is: for each BLOCK_N chunk, load `page_idx[0:BLOCK_N]` then load KV from scattered pages. On Blackwell, the L1 cache is 128KB per SM. With 16 tokens * 4 KV heads * 2 * 64 bytes = 8192 bytes for D=64, or 16 tokens * 4 KV heads * 2 * 128 bytes = 16384 bytes for D=128. This fits easily in L1. The issue is that each warp accesses a different set of pages, and the page indices are not known at compile time. The hardware must issue separate load instructions for each page, or rely on the L1 cache to coalesce. Wait, actually Triton might be able to coalesce the loads if the page indices are the same for consecutive threads. Let me think about how Triton compiles this. In Triton, `tl.load` with a block pointer or tensor of pointers generates a vector load instruction. If the pointers are contiguous, it uses a single wide load. If they're scattered, it uses multiple instructions or relies on the cache. For the page-table gather: ```python page_idx = tl.load(block_tables + cur_batch * stride_bt0 + (offs_n // PAGE_SIZE), mask=mask_n, other=0) ``` This loads BLOCK_N page indices. Then for each token position: ```python k = tl.load(KV_Cache + page_idx * stride_kv0 + token_in_page * stride_kv1 + cur_kv_head * stride_kv2 + offs_d_k[None, :], ...) ``` If `page_idx` is the same for all tokens in a BLOCK_N chunk (which happens when BLOCK_N <= PAGE_SIZE and all tokens are in the same page), then `page_idx * stride_kv0` is the same base address for all tokens. And `token_in_page * stride_kv1` gives offsets of `0, stride_kv1, 2*stride_kv1, ...`. If `stride_kv1` is contiguous in memory (which it is, since it's the inner dimension), then the K/V loads are contiguous across the token dimension. But when BLOCK_N spans multiple pages, `page_idx` varies, and the loads become scattered. In my kernel, BLOCK_N=16 and PAGE_SIZE=16. So a BLOCK_N chunk always spans exactly one page. This means all tokens in a BLOCK_N chunk are in the same page! Wait, but `token_in_page = offs_n % PAGE_SIZE`. If `start_n` is not a multiple of PAGE_SIZE, then `offs_n` spans two pages. For example, if `start_n = 8` and `BLOCK_N = 16` and `PAGE_SIZE = 16`, then tokens 8-15 are in page 0 and tokens 16-23 are in page 1. But wait, my kernel computes: ```python page_idx = tl.load(block_tables + cur_batch * stride_bt0 + (offs_n // PAGE_SIZE), mask=mask_n, other=0) ``` If `offs_n = [8, 9, ..., 23]`, then `offs_n // PAGE_SIZE = [0, 0, ..., 0, 1, 1, ..., 1]`. So `page_idx` is not uniform. But Triton may handle this by broadcasting or generating multiple loads. If the page indices are not all the same, the load of `KV_Cache` becomes a gather. However, looking at my loop: `for start_n in range(split_kv_start, split_kv_end, BLOCK_N)`. The `split_kv_start` is `kv_len_per_split * split_kv_id`, which may not be a multiple of PAGE_SIZE. For example, with seq_len=2000, num_kv_splits=16, kv_len_per_split=125. For split 0, start_n=0, 0 is a multiple of PAGE_SIZE (16). For split 1, start_n=125, 125 // 16 = 7 remainder 13. So the first BLOCK_N=16 chunk covers tokens 125-140, which spans pages 7 and 8. The next chunk covers 141-156, spanning pages 8 and 9, etc. So many BLOCK_N chunks span two pages, causing scattered loads. What if I align BLOCK_N to PAGE_SIZE? Or use BLOCK_N = PAGE_SIZE = 16, but ensure that `start_n` is always aligned to PAGE_SIZE boundaries? I can't easily control `split_kv_start` because it's determined by `kv_len_per_split * split_kv_id`. But I could change the loop to iterate over whole pages instead of fixed BLOCK_N chunks. Actually, what if I iterate over pages instead of tokens? Like: ```python for page_offset in range(num_pages_in_split): page_idx = tl.load(block_tables + ...) # Load all tokens in this page: (PAGE_SIZE, HEAD_DIM) page_k = tl.load(KV_Cache + page_idx * stride_kv0 + tl.arange(0, PAGE_SIZE) * stride_kv1 + ...) # Then compute qk for all tokens in page ``` This would ensure that all tokens in a page are loaded contiguously. But it would require masking tokens beyond the split boundary. Let me try this: instead of BLOCK_N=16 tokens, process one page (16 tokens) at a time, but load the entire page contiguously. Wait, I already do BLOCK_N=16 which equals PAGE_SIZE=16. The issue is that `start_n` may not be page-aligned. If I ensure `start_n` is always page-aligned, then each BLOCK_N chunk loads exactly one page. How to ensure alignment? I can round `split_kv_start` down to the nearest page boundary, and round `split_kv_end` up to the nearest page boundary. Then mask out tokens outside the actual split range. But this means some splits will load extra tokens from adjacent splits, causing wasted memory bandwidth. However, the benefit of contiguous page loads might outweigh the cost. Alternatively, I can make `kv_len_per_split` a multiple of PAGE_SIZE. For example: ```python pages_per_split = max(1, (total_pages + NUM_KV_SPLITS - 1) // NUM_KV_SPLITS) kv_len_per_split = pages_per_split * PAGE_SIZE ``` Then each split processes a whole number of pages. The last split might process fewer actual tokens, but the loads are page-aligned. This could significantly improve memory coalescing. Let me test this idea.

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math
import statistics

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

shape = shapes.SHAPES[4]
apply_shape(shape)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]

sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(64)

# Test with aligned splits
for num_kv_splits in [4, 8, 16, 20]:
    for _ in range(10):
        solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], sm_scale, 16, num_kv_splits)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    
    times = []
    for _ in range(30):
        _l2_scratch.zero_()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], sm_scale, 16, num_kv_splits)
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    
    print(f"splits={num_kv_splits}: ms={statistics.median(times):.3f}")

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math
import statistics

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

shape = shapes.SHAPES[4]
apply_shape(shape)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]

# Test 1: Direct call
sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(64)
num_kv_splits = 20

for _ in range(15):
    solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], sm_scale, 16, num_kv_splits)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

times1 = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], sm_scale, 16, num_kv_splits)
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times1.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Direct: ms={statistics.median(times1):.3f}")

# Test 2: Model.call with identical args
sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()
print(f"Model.scale={sol_model.scale}, direct_scale={sm_scale}, match={sol_model.scale==sm_scale}")

for _ in range(15):
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model.forward(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
torch.cuda.synchronize()

times2 = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model.forward(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times2.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Model.forward: ms={statistics.median(times2):.3f}")

# Test 3: Model.__call__ with identical args
for _ in range(15):
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
torch.cuda.synchronize()

times3 = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    with torch.no_grad():
        sol_model(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times3.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Model.__call__: ms={statistics.median(times3):.3f}")

# Test 4: Model.__call__ without torch.no_grad
for _ in range(15):
    sol_model(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
torch.cuda.synchronize()

times4 = []
for _ in range(30):
    _l2_scratch.zero_()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
    s.record()
    sol_model(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3])
    e.record()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times4.append(s.elapsed_time(e))

print(f"Model.__call__ (no no_grad): ms={statistics.median(times4):.3f}")
EOF

import sys
sys.path.insert(0, '.')
import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math
import statistics
import random

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]
    reference.SEQ_LEN = shape["seq_len"]
    reference.PAGE_SIZE = shape["page_size"]

device = torch.device("cuda:0")
_l2_scratch = torch.empty(128 * 1024 * 1024 // 4, dtype=torch.float32, device="cuda")

shape = shapes.SHAPES[4]
apply_shape(shape)

torch.manual_seed(2026)
init_args = reference.get_init_inputs()
inputs = [t.to(device) for t in reference.get_inputs()]

sm_scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(64)
num_kv_splits = 20

def run_test(name, fn, warmup=15, iters=30):
    for _ in range(warmup):
        with torch.no_grad():
            fn()
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    times = []
    for _ in range(iters):
        _l2_scratch.zero_()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        s = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        e = torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)
        s.record()
        with torch.no_grad():
            fn()
        e.record()
        torch.cuda.synchronize()
        times.append(s.elapsed_time(e))
    print(f"{name}: ms={statistics.median(times):.3f}")

sol_model = solution.Model(*init_args).to(device).eval()

# Run tests in random order multiple times
for i in range(5):
    order = ['direct', 'forward', 'call']
    random.shuffle(order)
    print(f"\nRun {i+1}, order: {order}")
    for name in order:
        if name == 'direct':
            run_test('direct', lambda: solution.paged_attention_decode(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3], sm_scale, 16, num_kv_splits))
        elif name == 'forward':
            run_test('forward', lambda: sol_model.forward(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3]))
        else:
            run_test('call', lambda: sol_model(inputs[0], inputs[1], inputs[2], inputs[3]))
EOF

Interesting! The results show that whichever test runs FIRST in a given run tends to be slower. This is a classic "first call after synchronization" effect or "cold cache" effect. - Run 1: direct first -> 0.044 ms (slow), call -> 0.035, forward -> 0.034 - Run 2: forward first -> 0.034 ms, call -> 0.043 (slow), direct -> 0.033 - Run 3: forward first -> 0.034 ms, direct -> 0.033, call -> 0.047 (slow) - Run 4: forward first -> 0.035 ms, call -> 0.035, direct -> 0.033 - Run 5: forward first -> 0.041 ms (slow-ish), direct -> 0.035, call -> 0.034 So the "slow" result isn't specific to `forward` or `call` or `direct`. It's whichever one runs first after the previous test's synchronization and L2 flush. The actual stable performance is: - direct: ~0.033 ms - call: ~0.034-0.035 ms - forward: ~0.034-0.035 ms And the "first call" penalty is about 0.01 ms. This is probably because the first call after `torch.cuda.synchronize()` has to wait for the GPU to come out of an idle state, or there's some launch overhead. In `benchmark.py`, each timed iteration does: ```python l2_flush.zero_() torch.cuda.synchronize() start_event.record() fn(*inputs) end_event.record() torch.cuda.synchronize() ``` So the first call after each L2 flush and synchronize might have this penalty. But the benchmark takes the median over many iterations, which should filter out the first-call penalty if it's consistent. Actually, in my test_random_order.py, I also do `_l2_scratch.zero_()` and `torch.cuda.synchronize()` before each timed call. So the "first call" penalty should apply to all calls equally, not just the first one in a run. Wait, but within a single run (e.g., Run 1), the three tests are sequential. After `run_test('direct', ...)` finishes, the GPU might still be warm. Then `run_test('call', ...)` starts with another `_l2_scratch.zero_()` and `synchronize()`, but the Triton kernel might still be in some cache. Hmm, the variability seems to be about which Triton kernel configuration gets "cached" or "primed" first. Actually, looking more carefully: `run_test` does 15 warmup calls, then 30 timed calls. The timed calls are all identical and should be consistent within a test. But the first test in each run (e.g., direct in Run 1) gives 0.044 ms, while the same direct call in Run 2 (third test) gives 0.033 ms. This suggests that something about running the previous tests affects the state of the GPU or Triton cache, making subsequent calls faster. Wait, could it be that the `solution.Model` instance in `call` and `forward` tests causes some CUDA context or module state to be initialized, which then benefits the `direct` test when it runs later? Or maybe it's simpler: Triton kernel compilation happens lazily on the first call. If I call `paged_attention_decode` directly first, it compiles the kernel. Then when I call `sol_model`, it uses the already-compiled kernel. But the compilation happens in the background and might overlap with the first few timed calls. Actually, in `run_test`, I do 15 warmup calls before timing. That should be enough for compilation. But wait, in test_random_order.py, all three tests share the SAME `sol_model` instance. And the `direct` test doesn't use `sol_model` at all. But `sol_model` was created before any test runs. I think the most likely explanation is that GPU clock frequencies are dynamic. After a period of inactivity (or between test runs), the GPU might downclock. The first test after a gap might run at a lower frequency. Let me check if GPU frequency scaling is active.

reasoning (2996 chars)
The GPU is currently at 187 MHz SM clock, which is very low (idle state). The max is 3090 MHz. This confirms that the GPU downclocks when idle. When we run a kernel, the GPU takes some time to boost back up to full clock. The first few kernel launches after idle might run at lower clock speeds, explaining the "first call penalty". In `benchmark.py`, the L2 flush + synchronize between iterations might cause the GPU to drop clocks slightly, but the median over many iterations should capture the steady-state performance. However, if the benchmark runs quickly (e.g., 0.03-0.05 ms per iteration), the GPU might not have time to reach max clocks between iterations, especially with L2 flushes. This is a known issue with microbenchmarking on modern GPUs. To get stable results, we need to either: 1. Disable GPU clock scaling (requires root, not possible) 2. Add a "burn-in" period before timing 3. Use a dummy kernel to keep the GPU awake between iterations In benchmark.py, they do: ```python for _ in range(warmup): fn(*inputs) torch.cuda.synchronize() times = [] for _ in range(iters): l2_flush.zero_() torch.cuda.synchronize() start.record() fn(*inputs) end.record() torch.cuda.synchronize() times.append(start.elapsed_time(end)) ``` The L2 flush and synchronize between each iteration might be causing the GPU to drop clocks. But the median should still be OK if the clock behavior is consistent. Anyway, the key takeaway is: the kernel itself is running at ~0.033-0.035 ms for direct calls on shape 4. The Model overhead is minimal (~0.001 ms). The "0.051 ms" I saw earlier was likely due to GPU clock scaling or first-call effects. So the actual kernel performance for shape 4 is ~0.034 ms direct, ~0.035 ms via Model. Now, let me compute the bandwidth utilization at 0.035 ms: - Bytes: 16.4 MB - Time: 0.035 ms = 35 microseconds - BW: 16.4 MB / 35 us = 468.6 GB/s - Peak: 1800 GB/s - Fraction: 468.6 / 1800 = 26% Hmm, that's still low. But for a memory-bound kernel with page-table indirection, 26% might be reasonable. import sys

import torch
import reference
import solution
import shapes
import math
import statistics

def apply_shape(shape):
    reference.BATCH = shape["batch"]
    reference.NUM_HEADS = shape["num_heads"]
    reference.NUM_KV_HEADS = shape["num_kv_heads"]
    reference.HEAD_DIM = shape["head_dim"]

    with torch.no_grad():

    e.record()

ms = statistics.median(times)
B, H, D = inputs[0].shape
Hkv = sol_model.num_kv_heads
L = sol_model.seq_len
bytes_moved = 2 * B * L * Hkv * D * 2 + B * H * D * 2 + B * H * D * 2  # KV + Q + Out in bytes
bw = bytes_moved / (ms / 1000) / 1e9
print(f"Shape 0: ms={ms:.3f}, BW={bw:.1f} GB/s, fraction={bw/1800:.3f}")

Now, I noticed in the current solution.py that there's a `_fwd_kernel_stage1` that iterates over pages (not tokens), loading entire pages at a time. This is the optimized version from the compaction context. But wait, I also see that there's a `_fwd_grouped_kernel_stage1` mentioned in the compaction output, but the current solution.py only has `_fwd_kernel_stage1`. Let me re-read the current solution.py more carefully. Actually, looking at the current solution.py: - `_fwd_kernel_stage1` iterates over pages (`for page_idx in range(start_page, end_page)`) and loads `offs_token_in_page = tl.arange(0, PAGE_SIZE)`. - The grid is `(batch, triton.cdiv(head_num, min(BLOCK_H, kv_group_num)), num_kv_splits)`. - BLOCK_H is passed as a parameter to `_decode_grouped_att_m_fwd` with default 8. This kernel handles grouped query attention by having `heads_per_kv = kv_group_num // BLOCK_H` (wait, it's `tl.cdiv(kv_group_num, BLOCK_H)`). Wait, `cur_kv_head = cur_head_id // heads_per_kv`. And `cur_head = cur_head_id * VALID_BLOCK_H + tl.arange(0, BLOCK_H)`. If `kv_group_num=4` and `BLOCK_H=8`, then `heads_per_kv = tl.cdiv(4, 8) = 1`. So `cur_kv_head = cur_head_id // 1 = cur_head_id`. And `VALID_BLOCK_H = 4` (since `kv_group_num < BLOCK_H`). So `cur_head = cur_head_id * 4 + [0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7]`, but `mask_h` only keeps `[0,1,2,3]`. This means each block computes 4 query heads, but the warp still processes 8 threads for heads (with 4 masked out). This wastes some compute but is fine. For `kv_group_num=8` and `BLOCK_H=8`, `heads_per_kv = 1`, `VALID_BLOCK_H = 8`. Each block computes 8 query heads. For `kv_group_num=4` and `BLOCK_H=16`, `heads_per_kv = 1`, `VALID_BLOCK_H = 4`. Each block computes 4 query heads but with 16-wide tiles. The current kernel seems correct. It iterates over pages, which should be good for memory coalescing.
